</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:CRISPR" title="Template:CRISPR"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:CRISPR" title="Template talk:CRISPR"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:CRISPR" title="Special:EditPage/Template:CRISPR"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:GRNA-Cas9.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/GRNA-Cas9.svg/250px-GRNA-Cas9.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/GRNA-Cas9.svg/500px-GRNA-Cas9.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2195" data-file-height="1470" /></a><figcaption>CRISPR-Cas9</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>CRISPR gene editing</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span></span>/</a></span></span>; pronounced like "crisper"; an abbreviation for "clustered regularly interspaced short palindromic repeats") is a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> technique in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a> by which the <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a> of living organisms may be modified. It is based on a simplified version of the bacterial <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>-<a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a> antiviral defense system. By delivering the Cas9 <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nuclease</a> complexed with a synthetic <a href="/wiki/Guide_RNA" title="Guide RNA">guide RNA</a> (gRNA) into a cell, the cell's genome can be cut at a desired location, allowing existing genes to be removed or new ones added <i><a href="/wiki/In_vivo" title="In vivo">in vivo</a>("within the living")</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bak&#95;2018&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bak_2018-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The technique is considered highly significant in <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> and medicine as it enables <i>in vivo</i> genome editing and is considered exceptionally precise, cost-effective, and efficient. It can be used in the creation of new medicines, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural products</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetically modified organisms">genetically modified organisms</a>, or as a means of controlling <a href="/wiki/Pathogens" class="mw-redirect" title="Pathogens">pathogens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pest_control" title="Pest control">pests</a>. It also offers potential in the treatment of inherited <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic diseases">genetic diseases</a> as well as diseases arising from <a href="/wiki/Somatic_mutation" title="Somatic mutation">somatic mutations</a>, such as cancer. However, its use in <a href="/wiki/Human_germline_engineering" title="Human germline engineering">human germline genetic modification</a> is highly controversial. The development of this technique earned <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Doudna" title="Jennifer Doudna">Jennifer Doudna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emmanuelle_Charpentier" title="Emmanuelle Charpentier">Emmanuelle Charpentier</a> the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 2020.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cohen&#95;2020&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen_2020-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third researcher group that shared the <a href="/wiki/Kavli_Prize" title="Kavli Prize">Kavli Prize</a> for the same discovery,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> led by <a href="/wiki/Virginijus_%C5%A0ik%C5%A1nys#Publication_of_CRISPR-Cas" title="Virginijus Šikšnys">Virginijus Šikšnys</a>, was not awarded the Nobel Prize.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owens&#95;2020&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owens_2020-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Working like genetic scissors, the Cas9 nuclease opens both strands of the targeted <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> sequence to introduce the modification by one of two methods. Knock-in mutations, facilitated via <a href="/wiki/Homology_directed_repair" title="Homology directed repair">homology-directed repair</a> (HDR), are the traditional pathway of targeted genomic editing approaches.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bak&#95;2018&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bak_2018-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This allows for the introduction of targeted <a href="/wiki/DNA_repair" title="DNA repair">DNA damage and repair</a>. HDR employs the use of similar DNA sequences to drive the repair of the break via the incorporation of exogenous DNA to function as the repair template.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bak&#95;2018&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bak_2018-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This method relies on the periodic and isolated occurrence of DNA damage at the target site in order for the repair to commence. Knock-out mutations caused by CRISPR-Cas9 result from the repair of the double-stranded break by means of <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining</a> (NHEJ) or <a href="/wiki/POLQ" title="POLQ">POLQ/polymerase theta</a>-mediated end-joining (TMEJ). These end-joining pathways can often result in random deletions or insertions at the repair site, which may disrupt or alter gene functionality. Therefore, genomic engineering with CRISPR-Cas9 enables researchers to generate targeted random gene disruption.
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing" title="Genome editing">genome editing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Eukaryote" title="Eukaryote">eukaryotic cells</a> has been possible using various methods since the 1980s, the methods employed have proven to be inefficient and impractical for large-scale implementation. With the discovery of CRISPR and specifically the Cas9 nuclease molecule, efficient and highly selective editing became possible. Cas9, derived from the bacterial species <i><a href="/wiki/Streptococcus_pyogenes" title="Streptococcus pyogenes">Streptococcus pyogenes</a>,</i> has facilitated targeted genomic modification in eukaryotic cells by enabling a reliable method of creating a targeted break at a specific location as designated by the crRNA and tracrRNA guide strands.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers can insert Cas9 and template RNA with ease in order to <a href="/wiki/Gene_silencing" title="Gene silencing">silence</a> or cause <a href="/wiki/Point_mutation" title="Point mutation">point mutations</a> at specific <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">loci</a>. This has proven invaluable for quick and efficient <a href="/wiki/Gene_mapping" title="Gene mapping">mapping</a> of genomic models and biological processes associated with various genes in a variety of eukaryotes. Newly engineered variants of the Cas9 nuclease that significantly reduce off-target activity have been developed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR-Cas9 genome editing techniques have many potential applications. The use of the CRISPR-Cas9-gRNA complex for genome editing<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ledford&#95;2016&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ledford_2016-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> was the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">AAAS</a>'s choice for <a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_of_the_Year" title="Breakthrough of the Year">Breakthrough of the Year</a> in 2015.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since 2015, CRISPR has been experimentally investigated on non-viable human <a href="/wiki/Embryo" title="Embryo">embryos</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2019, the first humans were born from genome-edited embryos using the CRISPR technique, as a result of the controversial <a href="/wiki/He_Jiankui_affair" title="He Jiankui affair">He Jiankui affair</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NAT-201905203&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-201905203-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several <a href="/wiki/Bioethical" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioethical">bioethical</a> concerns have been raised about the prospect of using CRISPR for <a href="/wiki/Germline" title="Germline">germline</a> editing, especially the potential enabling of human <a href="/wiki/Eugenics" title="Eugenics">eugenics</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2023, the first drug making use of CRISPR gene editing, <a href="/wiki/Exagamglogene_autotemcel" title="Exagamglogene autotemcel">Exagamglogene autotemcel</a>, sold under the brand name "Casgevy", was officially approved for use in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, to cure <a href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle-cell disease">sickle-cell disease</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-casgevy&#95;bbc&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casgevy_bbc-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-casgevy&#95;gov&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casgevy_gov-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On 2 December 2023, the Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a> became the second country in the world to approve the use of Casgevy to treat <a href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle-cell anemia">sickle-cell anemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On December 8, 2023, Casgevy received approval for use in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-casgevy&#95;fda&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casgevy_fda-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_methods">Other methods</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Other methods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the early 2000s, German researchers began developing <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger_nuclease" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc finger nuclease">zinc finger nucleases</a> (ZFNs), synthetic proteins whose <a href="/wiki/DNA-binding_domains" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA-binding domains">DNA-binding domains</a> enable them to create double-stranded breaks in DNA at specific points. ZFNs have a higher precision and the advantage of being smaller than Cas9, but ZFNs are not as commonly used as CRISPR-based methods. In 2010, synthetic nucleases called <a href="/wiki/Transcription_activator-like_effector_nuclease" title="Transcription activator-like effector nuclease">transcription activator-like effector nucleases</a> (TALENs) provided an easier way to target a double-stranded break to a specific location on the DNA strand. Both zinc finger nucleases and TALENs require the design and creation of a custom protein for each targeted DNA sequence, which is a much more difficult and time-consuming process than that of designing guide RNAs. CRISPRs are much easier to design because the process requires synthesizing only a short RNA sequence, a procedure that is already widely used for many other molecular biology techniques (e.g., creating <a href="/wiki/Oligonucleotide" title="Oligonucleotide">oligonucleotide</a> <a href="/wiki/Primer_(molecular_biology)" title="Primer (molecular biology)">primers</a>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MIT&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas methods such as <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a> (RNAi) do not fully suppress gene function, CRISPR, <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger_nuclease" class="mw-redirect" title="Zinc finger nuclease">ZFNs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transcription_activator-like_effector_nuclease" title="Transcription activator-like effector nuclease">TALENs</a> provide full, irreversible <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">gene knockout</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Heidenreich&#95;2016&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heidenreich_2016-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR can also target several DNA sites simultaneously simply by introducing different gRNAs. In addition, the costs of employing CRISPR are relatively low.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Heidenreich&#95;2016&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heidenreich_2016-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Discovery">Discovery</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Discovery"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2005, Alexander Bolotin at the French National Institute for Agricultural Research (INRA) discovered a CRISPR locus that contained novel Cas genes, significantly one that encoded a large protein known as Cas9.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Broad&#95;Institute&#95;2015&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad_Institute_2015-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, Eugene Koonin at the US <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Biotechnology_Information" title="National Center for Biotechnology Information">National Center for Biotechnology Information</a>, NCBI, proposed an explanation as to how CRISPR cascades as a bacterial immune system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Broad&#95;Institute&#95;2015&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad_Institute_2015-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, Philippe Horvath at Danisco France SAS displayed experimentally how CRISPR systems are an adaptive immune system, and integrate new phage DNA into the CRISPR array, which is how they fight off the next wave of attacking phage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Broad&#95;Institute&#95;2015&#95;24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad_Institute_2015-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2012, the research team, led by professor <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Doudna" title="Jennifer Doudna">Jennifer Doudna</a> (<a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>) and professor <a href="/wiki/Emmanuelle_Charpentier" title="Emmanuelle Charpentier">Emmanuelle Charpentier</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ume%C3%A5_University" title="Umeå University">Umeå University</a>), was the first group of individuals to identify, disclose, and file a patent application for the CRISPR-Cas9 system needed to edit DNA.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Broad&#95;Institute&#95;2015&#95;24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad_Institute_2015-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They also published their finding that CRISPR-<a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a> could be programmed with RNA to edit genomic DNA, now considered one of the most significant discoveries in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_biology" title="History of biology">history of biology</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Patents_and_commercialization">Patents and commercialization</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Patents and commercialization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As of November&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, SAGE Labs (part of <a href="/wiki/Horizon_Discovery" title="Horizon Discovery">Horizon Discovery</a> group) had <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_right" title="Exclusive right">exclusive rights</a> from one of those companies to produce and sell genetically engineered rats and non-exclusive rights for mouse and rabbit models.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Thermo_Fisher_Scientific" title="Thermo Fisher Scientific">Thermo Fisher Scientific</a> had licensed intellectual property from ToolGen to develop CRISPR reagent kits.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As of December&#160;2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Patent_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Patent rights">patent rights</a> to CRISPR were contested. Several companies were formed to develop related drugs and research tools.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-TR&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TR-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As companies ramped up financing, doubts as to whether CRISPR could be quickly monetized were raised.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Feng_Zhang" title="Feng Zhang">Feng Zhang</a> of the Broad Institute of MIT and Harvard and nine others were awarded US patent number 8,697,359<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> over the use of CRISPR–Cas9 gene editing in eukaryotes. Although Charpentier and Doudna (referred to as CVC) were credited for the conception of CRISPR, the Broad Institute was the first to achieve a "reduction to practice" according to patent judges Sally Gardner Lane, James T. Moore, and Deborah Katz.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first set of patents was awarded to the Broad team in 2015, prompting attorneys for the CVC group to request the first interference proceeding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In February 2017, the US Patent Office ruled on a <a href="/wiki/Patent_interference" class="mw-redirect" title="Patent interference">patent interference</a> case brought by the University of California with respect to patents issued to the <a href="/wiki/Broad_Institute" title="Broad Institute">Broad Institute</a>, and found that the Broad patents, with claims covering the application of CRISPR-Cas9 in eukaryotic cells, were distinct from the inventions claimed by the University of California.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Shortly after, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California" title="University of California">University of California</a> filed an appeal of this ruling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Potenza&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Potenza-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Buhr&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buhr-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2019, the second interference dispute was opened. This was in response to patent applications made by CVC that required the appeals board to determine the original inventor of the technology. The USPTO ruled in March 2022 against UC, stating that the Broad Institute was first to file. The decision affected many of the licensing agreements for the CRISPR editing technology that was licensed from UC Berkeley. UC stated its intent to appeal the USPTO's ruling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Recent_events">Recent events</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Recent events"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In March 2017, the European Patent Office (EPO) announced its intention to allow claims for editing all types of cells to the <a href="/wiki/Max_Planck_Society" title="Max Planck Society">Max-Planck Institute</a> in Berlin, the University of California, and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Vienna" title="University of Vienna">University of Vienna</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Philippidis&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philippidis-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Akst&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akst-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in August 2017, the EPO announced its intention to allow CRISPR claims in a patent application that MilliporeSigma had filed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Philippidis&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philippidis-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of August&#160;2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the patent situation in Europe was complex, with MilliporeSigma, ToolGen, Vilnius University, and Harvard contending for claims, along with the University of California and Broad.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In July 2018, the <a href="/wiki/ECJ" class="mw-redirect" title="ECJ">ECJ</a> ruled that <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">gene editing</a> for plants was a sub-category of <a href="/wiki/GMO_foods" class="mw-redirect" title="GMO foods">GMO foods</a> and therefore that the CRISPR technique would henceforth be regulated in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> by their rules and regulations for <a href="/wiki/GMO" class="mw-redirect" title="GMO">GMOs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eucrispr&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eucrispr-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2020, a US trial showed safe CRISPR gene editing on three <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> patients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2020, researchers Emmanuelle Charpentier and Jennifer Doudna were awarded the Nobel Prize in Chemistry for their work in this field.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They made history as the first two women to share this award without a male contributor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owens&#95;2020&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owens_2020-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In June 2021, the first, small <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trial</a> of <a href="/wiki/Intravenous_therapy" title="Intravenous therapy">intravenous</a> CRISPR gene editing in humans concluded with promising results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-CRISPR-Cas9&#95;In&#95;Vivo&#95;Gene&#95;Editing&#95;fo&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRISPR-Cas9_In_Vivo_Gene_Editing_fo-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In September 2021, the first CRISPR-edited food went on public sale in Japan. Tomatoes <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">were genetically modified</a> for around five times the normal amount of possibly calming<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR was first applied in tomatoes in 2014.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2021, it was reported that the first CRISPR-gene-edited marine animal/<a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a> and second set of CRISPR-edited food has gone on public sale in Japan: two fish of which one species grows to twice the size of natural specimens due to disruption of <a href="/wiki/Leptin" title="Leptin">leptin</a>, which controls appetite, and the other grows to 1.2 times the natural average size with the same amount of food due to disabled <a href="/wiki/Myostatin" title="Myostatin">myostatin</a>, which inhibits <a href="/wiki/Muscle_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Muscle growth">muscle growth</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A 2022 study has found that knowing more about CRISPR tomatoes had a strong effect on the participants' preference. "Almost half of the 32 participants from Germany who are scientists demonstrated constant choices, while the majority showed increased willingness to buy CRISPR tomatoes, mostly non-scientists."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2021, UC Berkeley announced their intent to auction <a href="/wiki/Non-fungible_tokens" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-fungible tokens">non-fungible tokens</a> of both the patent for CRISPR gene editing as well as <a href="/wiki/Cancer_immunotherapy" title="Cancer immunotherapy">cancer immunotherapy</a>. However, the university would, in this case, retain ownership of the patents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 85% of the funds gathered through the sale of the collection named <i>The Fourth Pillar</i> were to be used to finance research.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It sold in June 2022 for 22 Ether, which was around <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>54,000 at the time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jonesnature482&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jonesnature482-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2023, the United Kingdom's <a href="/wiki/Medicines_and_Healthcare_products_Regulatory_Agency" title="Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency">Medicines and Healthcare products Regulatory Agency</a> (MHRA) became the first in the world to approve the use of the first drug based on CRISPR gene editing, Casgevy, to treat <a href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle-cell anemia">sickle-cell anemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a>. Casgevy, or <a href="/wiki/Exagamglogene_autotemcel" title="Exagamglogene autotemcel">exagamglogene autotemcel</a>, directly acts on the genes of the stem cells inside the patient's bones, having them produce healthy red blood cells. This treatment thus avoids the need for regular, costly blood transfusions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-casgevy&#95;bbc&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casgevy_bbc-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-casgevy&#95;gov&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-casgevy_gov-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 2 December 2023, the Kingdom of Bahrain's <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Regulatory_Authority" class="mw-redirect" title="National Health Regulatory Authority">National Health Regulatory Authority</a> (NHRA) became the second in the world to approve the use of Casgevy to treat <a href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Sickle-cell anemia">sickle-cell anemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto1&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2023, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">FDA</a> approved the first gene therapy in the US to treat patients with Sickle Cell Disease (SCD). The FDA approved two milestone treatments, Casgevy and Lyfgenia, representing the first cell-based gene therapies for the treatment of SCD.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 16 February 2025, the Kingdom of Bahrain's announced the successful completion of treatment with Casgevy for the world's first sickle cell disease patient outside of the United States at the Royal Medical Services- Bahrain Oncology Center, located at <a href="/wiki/King_Hamad_University_Hospital" title="King Hamad University Hospital">King Hamad University Hospital</a> (KHUH),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and was congratulated by the World Health Organisation's Director General, <a href="/wiki/Tedros_Adhanom_Ghebreyesus" title="Tedros Adhanom Ghebreyesus">Tedros Adhanom Ghebreyesus</a> for the successful administration of the treatment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Genome_engineering">Genome engineering</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Genome engineering"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:DNA_Repair.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DNA_Repair.png/500px-DNA_Repair.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="433" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/DNA_Repair.png/960px-DNA_Repair.png 2x" data-file-width="3574" data-file-height="4073" /></a><figcaption>DNA repair after a double-stranded break</figcaption></figure>
<p>CRISPR-Cas9 genome editing uses a <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Cas_genes_and_CRISPR_subtypes" title="CRISPR">Type II</a> CRISPR system. This system includes a <a href="/wiki/Ribonucleoprotein" class="mw-redirect" title="Ribonucleoprotein">ribonucleoprotein</a> (RNP), consisting of <a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a>, crRNA, and tracrRNA, along with an optional DNA repair template.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CRISPR_overview_-_en.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/CRISPR_overview_-_en.svg/500px-CRISPR_overview_-_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="391" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/CRISPR_overview_-_en.svg/960px-CRISPR_overview_-_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1215" data-file-height="1250" /></a><figcaption>Overview of CRISPR-Cas9 plasmid construction</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Major_components">Major components</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Major components"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Component
</th>
<th>Function
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/List_of_RNAs" title="List of RNAs">crRNA</a>
</td>
<td>Contains the guide RNA that locates the correct segment of host DNA, along with a region that binds to <a href="/wiki/Trans-activating_crRNA" title="Trans-activating crRNA">tracrRNA</a> (generally in a <a href="/wiki/Stem-loop" title="Stem-loop">hairpin loop</a> form), forming an active complex.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Trans-activating_crRNA" title="Trans-activating crRNA">tracrRNA</a>
</td>
<td>Binds to <a href="/wiki/List_of_RNAs" title="List of RNAs">crRNA</a> and forms an active complex.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>sgRNA
</td>
<td>Single-guide RNAs are a combined RNA consisting of a <a href="/wiki/Trans-activating_crRNA" title="Trans-activating crRNA">tracrRNA</a> and at least one <a href="/wiki/List_of_RNAs" title="List of RNAs">crRNA</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a> (<a href="#Alternatives_to_Cas9">most commonly</a>)
</td>
<td>An enzyme whose active form is able to modify DNA. Many variants exist with different functions (i.e., single-strand nicking, double-strand breaking, DNA binding) due to each enzyme's DNA site recognition function.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Repair template
</td>
<td>DNA molecule used as a template in the host cell's DNA repair process, allowing insertion of a specific DNA sequence into the host segment broken by Cas9.
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><a href="/wiki/CRISPR-Cas9" class="mw-redirect" title="CRISPR-Cas9">CRISPR-Cas9</a> often employs <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmids</a> that code for the RNP components to <a href="/wiki/Transfection" title="Transfection">transfect</a> the target cells, or the RNP is assembled before addition to the cells via nucleofection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ran&#95;2013&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ran_2013-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The main components of this plasmid are displayed in the image and listed in the table. The crRNA is uniquely designed for each application, as this is the sequence that Cas9 uses to identify and directly bind to specific sequences within the host cell's DNA. The crRNA must bind only where editing is desired. The repair template is also uniquely designed for each application, as it must complement to some degree the DNA sequences on either side of the cut and also contain whatever sequence is desired for insertion into the host genome.
</p><p>Multiple crRNAs and the tracrRNA can be packaged together to form a single-guide RNA (sgRNA).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This sgRNA can be included alongside the gene that codes for the Cas9 protein and made into a plasmid in order to be transfected into cells. Many online tools are available to aid in designing effective sgRNA sequences.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CRISPR_transfection.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/CRISPR_transfection.png/500px-CRISPR_transfection.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="369" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/CRISPR_transfection.png/960px-CRISPR_transfection.png 2x" data-file-width="1224" data-file-height="1188" /></a><figcaption>Overview of the transfection and DNA cleaving by CRISPR-Cas9 (crRNA and tracrRNA are often joined as a single strand of RNA when designing a plasmid)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ran&#95;2013&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ran_2013-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Alternatives_to_Cas9">Alternatives to Cas9</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Alternatives to Cas9"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Cas_genes_and_CRISPR_subtypes" title="CRISPR">CRISPR §&#160;Cas genes and CRISPR subtypes</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:PAMs_of_different_CRISPR_nucleases.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/PAMs_of_different_CRISPR_nucleases.svg/500px-PAMs_of_different_CRISPR_nucleases.svg.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="467" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/PAMs_of_different_CRISPR_nucleases.svg/960px-PAMs_of_different_CRISPR_nucleases.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="531" data-file-height="653" /></a><figcaption>Different CRISPR DNA nucleases with their PAM and size</figcaption></figure>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Structure">Structure</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Structure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR-Cas9 offers a high degree of fidelity and relatively simple construction. It depends on two factors for its specificity: the target sequence and the <a href="/wiki/Protospacer_adjacent_motif" title="Protospacer adjacent motif">protospacer adjacent motif</a> (PAM) sequence. The target sequence is 20 bases long as part of each CRISPR locus in the crRNA array.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ran&#95;2013&#95;65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ran_2013-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A typical crRNA array has multiple unique target sequences. Cas9 proteins select the correct location on the host's genome by utilizing the sequence to bond with base pairs on the host DNA. The sequence is not part of the Cas9 protein and as a result is customizable and can be independently <a href="/wiki/Oligonucleotide_synthesis" title="Oligonucleotide synthesis">synthesized</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The PAM sequence on the host genome is recognized by Cas9. Cas9 cannot be easily modified to recognize a different PAM sequence. However, this is ultimately not too limiting, as it is typically a very short and nonspecific sequence that occurs frequently at many places throughout the genome (e.g. the SpCas9 PAM sequence is 5'-NGG-3' and in the human genome occurs roughly every 8 to 12 base pairs).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ran&#95;2013&#95;65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ran_2013-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Once these sequences have been assembled into a plasmid and transfected into cells, the Cas9 protein with the help of the crRNA finds the correct sequence in the host cell's DNA and – depending on the Cas9 variant – creates a single- or double-stranded break at the appropriate location in the DNA.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Properly spaced single-stranded breaks in the host DNA can trigger <a href="/wiki/Homology_directed_repair" title="Homology directed repair">homology directed repair</a>, which is less error-prone than the <a href="/wiki/Non-homologous_end_joining" title="Non-homologous end joining">non-homologous end joining</a> or <a href="/wiki/POLQ" title="POLQ">theta-mediated end joining</a> that typically follows a double-stranded break. Providing a DNA repair template allows for the insertion of a specific DNA sequence at an exact location within the genome. The repair template should extend 40 to 90 base pairs beyond the Cas9-induced DNA break.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ran&#95;2013&#95;65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ran_2013-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The goal is for the cell's native HDR process to utilize the provided repair template and thereby incorporate the new sequence into the genome. Once incorporated, this new sequence is now part of the cell's genetic material and passes into its daughter cells. Combined transient inhibition of NHEJ and TMEJ by a small molecule and siRNAs can increase HDR efficiency to up to 93% and simultaneously prevent off-target editing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceB&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Delivery">Delivery</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Delivery"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transfection" title="Transfection">Transfection</a></div>
<p>Delivery of Cas9, sgRNA, and associated complexes into cells can occur via viral and non-viral systems. <a href="/wiki/Electroporation" title="Electroporation">Electroporation</a> of DNA, RNA, or ribonucleocomplexes is a common technique, though it can result in harmful effects on the target cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lino&#95;2018&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lino_2018-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chemical transfection techniques utilizing <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipids</a> and peptides have also been used to introduce sgRNAs in complex with Cas9 into cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Li&#95;2018&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li_2018-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nanoparticle" title="Nanoparticle">Nanoparticle</a>-based delivery has also been used for transfection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yip&#95;2020&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yip_2020-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Types of cells that are more difficult to transfect (e.g., stem cells, neurons, and hematopoietic cells) require more efficient delivery systems, such as those based on <a href="/wiki/Lentivirus" title="Lentivirus">lentivirus</a> (LVs), <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae" title="Adenoviridae">adenovirus</a> (AdV), and <a href="/wiki/Adeno-associated_virus" title="Adeno-associated virus">adeno-associated virus</a> (AAV).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schmidt&#95;2015&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schmidt_2015-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Efficiency of CRISPR-Cas9 has been found to greatly increase when various components of the system including the entire CRISPR/Cas9 structure to Cas9-gRNA complexes delivered in assembled form rather than using transgenics.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has found particular value in <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crop" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetically modified crop">genetically modified crops</a> for mass commercialization.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since the host's replication machinery is not needed to produce these proteins, the chance of the recognizing sequence of the sgRNA is almost none, decreasing the chance of off-target effects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yip&#95;2020&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yip_2020-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Controlled_genome_editing">Controlled genome editing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Controlled genome editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Further improvements and variants of the CRISPR-Cas9 system have focused on introducing more control into its use. Specifically, the research aimed at improving this system includes improving its specificity, its efficiency, and the granularity of its editing power. Techniques can further be divided and classified by the component of the system they modify. These include using different variants or novel creations of the Cas protein, using an altogether different effector protein, modifying the sgRNA, or using an algorithmic approach to identify existing optimal solutions.
</p><p>Specificity is an important aspect to improve the CRISPR-Cas9 system because the off-target effects it generates have serious consequences for the genome of the cell and invokes caution for its use. Minimizing off-target effects is thus maximizing the safety of the system. Novel variations of Cas9 proteins that increase specificity include effector proteins with comparable efficiency and specificity to the original SpCas9 that are able to target the previously untargetable sequences and a variant that has virtually no off-target mutations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Research has also been conducted in engineering new Cas9 proteins, including some that partially replace RNA nucleotides in crRNA with DNA and a structure-guided Cas9 mutant generating procedure that all had reduced off-target effects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Iteratively truncated sgRNAs and highly stabilized gRNAs have been shown to also decrease off-target effects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Computational methods including machine learning have been used to predict the affinity of and create unique sequences for the system to maximize specificity for given targets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Several variants of CRISPR-Cas9 allow gene activation or genome editing with an external trigger such as light or small molecules.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oakes&#95;2016&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oakes_2016-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26857072&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26857072-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26996256&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26996256-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These include photoactivatable CRISPR systems developed by fusing light-responsive protein partners with an activator domain and a dCas9 for gene activation,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25664691&#95;108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25664691-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25619936&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25619936-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or by fusing similar light-responsive domains with two constructs of split-Cas9,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25713377&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25713377-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26076431&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26076431-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or by incorporating caged unnatural amino acids into Cas9,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25905628&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25905628-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or by modifying the guide RNAs with photocleavable complements for genome editing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid27554600&#95;113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27554600-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Methods to control genome editing with small molecules include an allosteric Cas9, with no detectable background editing, that will activate binding and cleavage upon the addition of <a href="/wiki/Afimoxifene" title="Afimoxifene">4-hydroxytamoxifen</a> (4-HT),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Oakes&#95;2016&#95;105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oakes_2016-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 4-HT responsive <a href="/wiki/Intein" class="mw-redirect" title="Intein">intein</a>-linked Cas9,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or a Cas9 that is 4-HT responsive when fused to four ERT2 domains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Intein-inducible split-Cas9 allows <a href="/wiki/Dimerization_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dimerization (chemistry)">dimerization</a> of Cas9 fragments<sup id="cite&#95;ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sirolimus" title="Sirolimus">rapamycin</a>-inducible split-Cas9 system developed by fusing two constructs of split-Cas9 with FRB and <a href="/wiki/FKBP" title="FKBP">FKBP</a> fragments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other studies have been able to induce transcription of Cas9 with a small molecule, <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">doxycycline</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Small molecules can also be used to improve homology directed repair,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> often by inhibiting the non-homologous end joining pathway and/or the theta-mediated end-joining pathway.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A system with the Cpf1 effector protein was created that is induced by small molecules VE-822 and AZD-7762.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ma&#95;2018&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_2018-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These systems allow conditional control of CRISPR activity for improved precision, efficiency, and spatiotemporal control. Spatiotemporal control is a form of removing off-target effects—only certain cells or parts of the organism may need to be modified, and thus light or small molecules can be used as a way to conduct this. Efficiency of the CRISPR-Cas9 system is also greatly increased by proper delivery of the DNA instructions for creating the proteins and necessary reagents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ma&#95;2018&#95;123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_2018-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR also utilizes single base-pair editing proteins to create specific edits at one or two bases in the target sequence. CRISPR/Cas9 was fused with specific enzymes that initially could only change C to T and G to A mutations and their reverse. This was accomplished eventually without requiring any DNA cleavage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the fusion of another enzyme, the base editing CRISPR-Cas9 system can also edit C to G and its reverse.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="CRISPR_screening">CRISPR screening</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: CRISPR screening"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The clustered regularly interspaced short palindromic repeats <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">(CRISPR)/Cas9</a> system is a gene-editing technology that can induce <a href="/wiki/Double-strand_break" class="mw-redirect" title="Double-strand break">double-strand breaks</a> (DSBs) anywhere guide ribonucleic acids (<a href="/wiki/Guide_RNA" title="Guide RNA">gRNA</a>) can bind with the <a href="/wiki/Protospacer_adjacent_motif" title="Protospacer adjacent motif">protospacer adjacent motif</a> (PAM) sequence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kurata&#95;2018&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurata_2018-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Single-strand nicks can also be induced by Cas9 active-site mutants,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> also known as Cas9 nickases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By simply changing the sequence of gRNA, the Cas9-endonuclease can be delivered to a gene of interest and induce DSBs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The efficiency of Cas9-endonuclease and the ease by which genes can be targeted led to the development of CRISPR-knockout (KO) libraries both for mouse and human cells, which can cover either specific gene sets of interest or the whole-genome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Agrotis&#95;2015&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agrotis_2015-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR screening helps scientists to create a systematic and high-throughput genetic perturbation within live model organisms. This genetic perturbation is necessary for fully understanding gene function and epigenetic regulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Genome-scale&#95;CRISPR-Cas9&#95;knockout&#95;a&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genome-scale_CRISPR-Cas9_knockout_a-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The advantage of pooled CRISPR libraries is that more genes can be targeted at once.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Knock-out libraries are created in a way to achieve equal representation and performance across all expressed gRNAs and carry an antibiotic or fluorescent selection marker that can be used to recover transduced cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kurata&#95;2018&#95;128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurata_2018-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are two <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> systems in CRISPR/Cas9 libraries. First, is all in one plasmid, where sgRNA and Cas9 are produced simultaneously in a transfected cell. Second, is a two-vector system: sgRNA and Cas9 plasmids are delivered separately.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Genome-scale&#95;CRISPR-Cas9&#95;knockout&#95;a&#95;134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genome-scale_CRISPR-Cas9_knockout_a-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is important to deliver thousands of unique sgRNAs-containing vectors to a single vessel of cells by viral transduction at low <a href="/wiki/Multiplicity_of_infection" title="Multiplicity of infection">multiplicity of infection</a> (MOI, typically at 0.1–0.6), it prevents the probability that an individual cell clone will get more than one type of sgRNA otherwise it can lead to incorrect assignment of <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a> to <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Agrotis&#95;2015&#95;132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agrotis_2015-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Once a pooled library is prepared it is necessary to carry out a deep sequencing (NGS, next generation sequencing) of PCR-amplified plasmid DNA in order to reveal abundance of sgRNAs. Cells of interest can be consequentially infected by the library and then selected according to the phenotype. There are 2 types of selection: negative and positive. By negative selection dead or slow growing cells are efficiently detected. It can identify survival-essential genes, which can further serve as candidates for molecularly targeted drugs. On the other hand, positive selection gives a collection of growth-advantage acquired populations by random mutagenesis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kurata&#95;2018&#95;128-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurata_2018-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After selection genomic DNA is collected and sequenced by NGS. Depletion or enrichment of sgRNAs is detected and compared to the original sgRNA library, annotated with the target gene that sgRNA corresponds to. Statistical analysis then identifies genes that are significantly likely to be relevant to the phenotype of interest.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Agrotis&#95;2015&#95;132-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Agrotis_2015-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p>Apart from knock-out there are also knock-down (CRISPRi) and activation (CRISPRa) libraries, which use the ability of proteolytically deactivated Cas9-fusion proteins (dCas9) to bind target DNA, which means that a gene of interest is not cut but is over-expressed or repressed. It made CRISPR/Cas9 system even more interesting in gene editing. Inactive dCas9 protein modulate gene expression by targeting dCas9-repressors or activators toward promoter or transcriptional start sites of target genes. For repressing genes Cas9 can be fused to KRAB effector domain that makes complex with gRNA, whereas CRISPRa utilizes dCas9 fused to different transcriptional activation domains, which are further directed by gRNA to promoter regions to upregulate expression.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Applications">Applications</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Disease_models">Disease models</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Disease models"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Cas9 genomic modification has allowed for the quick and efficient generation of <a href="/wiki/Transgene" title="Transgene">transgenic</a> models within the field of genetics. Cas9 can be easily introduced into the target cells along with sgRNA via plasmid transfection in order to model the spread of diseases and the cell's response to and defense against infection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dow&#95;2015&#95;139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dow_2015-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ability of Cas9 to be introduced <i>in vivo</i> allows for the creation of more accurate models of gene function and mutation effects, all while avoiding the off-target mutations typically observed with older methods of genetic engineering.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The CRISPR and Cas9 revolution in genomic modeling does not extend only to mammals. Traditional genomic models such as <i><a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">Drosophila melanogaster</a></i>, one of the first model organisms, have seen further refinement in their resolution with the use of Cas9.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dow&#95;2015&#95;139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dow_2015-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cas9 uses cell-specific promoters allowing a controlled use of the Cas9. Cas9 is an accurate method of treating diseases due to the targeting of the Cas9 enzyme only affecting certain cell types. The cells undergoing the Cas9 therapy can also be removed and reintroduced to provide amplified effects of the therapy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Doudna&#95;2016&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Doudna_2016-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR-Cas9 can be used to edit the DNA of organisms <i>in vivo</i> and to eliminate individual genes or even entire <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> from an organism at any point in its development. Chromosomes that have been successfully deleted <i>in vivo</i> using CRISPR techniques include the Y chromosome and X chromosome of adult lab mice and human chromosomes 14 and 21, in embryonic stem cell lines and <a href="/wiki/Aneuploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Aneuploid">aneuploid</a> mice respectively. This method might be useful for treating genetic disorders caused by abnormal numbers of chromosomes, such as <a href="/wiki/Down_syndrome" title="Down syndrome">Down syndrome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intersex" title="Intersex">intersex</a> disorders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid29178945&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid29178945-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Successful <i>in vivo</i> genome editing using CRISPR-Cas9 has been shown in numerous model organisms, including <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Javed&#95;2018&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Javed_2018-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiCarlo&#95;2013&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiCarlo_2013-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Giersch&#95;1017&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giersch_1017-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Candida_albicans" title="Candida albicans">Candida albicans</a>, Methanosarcina acetivorans</i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Raschmanová&#95;2018&#95;146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raschmanová_2018-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">Caenorhabditis elegans</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ma&#95;2015&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_2015-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Arabidopsis" title="Arabidopsis">Arabidopsis</a></i> spp.,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Khurshid&#95;2018&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khurshid_2018-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Danio_rerio" class="mw-redirect" title="Danio rerio">Danio rerio</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid30076892&#95;149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid30076892-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/House_mouse" title="House mouse">house mouse</a> (<i>mus musculus</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Singh&#95;2015&#95;150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh_2015-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Soni&#95;2018&#95;151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Soni_2018-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Successes have been achieved in the study of basic
