biology, in the creation of disease models,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ma&#95;2015&#95;147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ma_2015-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in the experimental treatment of disease models.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GaoTao2017&#95;153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GaoTao2017-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Concerns have been raised that <a href="/wiki/Off-target_genome_editing" title="Off-target genome editing">off-target effects</a> (editing of genes besides the ones intended) may confound the results of a CRISPR gene editing experiment (i.e. the observed phenotype change may not be due to modifying the target gene, but some other gene). Modifications to CRISPR have been made to minimize the possibility of off-target effects. Orthogonal CRISPR experiments are often recommended to confirm the results of a gene editing experiment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kadam&#95;2018&#95;154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kadam_2018-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kimberland&#95;2018&#95;155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kimberland_2018-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR simplifies the creation of <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism#Mammals" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms</a> for research which mimic disease or show what happens when a gene is <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockdown" title="Gene knockdown">knocked down</a> or mutated. CRISPR may be used at the <a href="/wiki/Germline" title="Germline">germline</a> level to create organisms in which the targeted gene is changed everywhere (i.e. in all cells/tissues/organs of a multicellular organism), or it may be used in non-germline cells to create local changes that only affect certain cell populations within the organism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25914022&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25914022-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25819765&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25819765-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid25868999&#95;158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid25868999-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR can be utilized to create human cellular models of disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For instance, when applied to human <a href="/wiki/Cell_potency" title="Cell potency">pluripotent stem cells</a>, CRISPR has been used to introduce targeted mutations in genes relevant to <a href="/wiki/Polycystic_kidney_disease" title="Polycystic kidney disease">polycystic kidney disease</a> (PKD) and <a href="/wiki/Focal_segmental_glomerulosclerosis" title="Focal segmental glomerulosclerosis">focal segmental glomerulosclerosis</a> (FSGS).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These CRISPR-modified pluripotent stem cells were subsequently grown into human kidney <a href="/wiki/Organoid" title="Organoid">organoids</a> that exhibited disease-specific phenotypes. Kidney <a href="/wiki/Organoid" title="Organoid">organoids</a> from stem cells with PKD mutations formed large, translucent cyst structures from kidney tubules. The cysts were capable of reaching macroscopic dimensions, up to one centimeter in diameter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kidney organoids with mutations in a gene linked to FSGS developed junctional defects between <a href="/wiki/Podocyte" title="Podocyte">podocytes</a>, the filtering cells affected in that disease. This was traced to the inability of podocytes to form microvilli between adjacent cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Importantly, these disease phenotypes were absent in control organoids of identical genetic background, but lacking the CRISPR modifications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A similar approach was taken to model <a href="/wiki/Long_QT_syndrome" title="Long QT syndrome">long QT syndrome</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_muscle_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiac muscle cell">cardiomyocytes</a> derived from pluripotent stem cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These CRISPR-generated cellular models, with isogenic controls, provide a new way to study human disease and test drugs.
</p><p>CRISPR editing can be used to define the molecular function of non-coding alleles associated with complex diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These alleles are ubiquitously associated with complex traits, and work by altering gene regulation in relevant cell-types. They are also very difficult to ascribe function too. Genome editing can be used to introduce individual variants into the genome, and then downstream molecular effects can be defined. This approach must be done in appropriate cell-types or cell lines for the variant of interest. Also, this approach can be confounded by off-target effects, low efficiency editing, and non-specific transcriptional effects of gene editing.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biomedicine">Biomedicine</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Biomedicine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR-Cas technology has been proposed as a treatment for multiple human diseases, especially those with a genetic cause.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its ability to modify specific DNA sequences makes it a tool with potential to fix disease-causing mutations. Early research in animal models suggest that therapies based on CRISPR technology have potential to treat a wide range of diseases,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> including cancer,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Immuno-Oncology&#95;News&#95;2018&#95;167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Immuno-Oncology_News_2018-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Progeria" title="Progeria">progeria</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> beta-thalassemia,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frangoul&#95;2021&#95;171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frangoul_2021-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_disease" title="Sickle cell disease">sickle cell disease</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frangoul&#95;2021&#95;171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frangoul_2021-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Haemophilia" title="Haemophilia">hemophilia</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Duchenne_muscular_dystrophy" title="Duchenne muscular dystrophy">Duchenne's muscular dystrophy</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Huntington%27s_disease" title="Huntington&#39;s disease">Huntington's disease</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wild-type_transthyretin_amyloid" title="Wild-type transthyretin amyloid">transthyretin amyloidosis</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-CRISPR-Cas9&#95;In&#95;Vivo&#95;Gene&#95;Editing&#95;fo&#95;47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRISPR-Cas9_In_Vivo_Gene_Editing_fo-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and heart disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR has also been used to cure <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> in mosquitos, which could eliminate the vector and the disease in humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR may also have applications in tissue engineering and regenerative medicine, such as by creating human blood vessels that lack expression of <a href="/wiki/MHC_class_II" title="MHC class II">MHC class II</a> proteins, which often cause transplant rejection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, clinical trials to cure <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_disease" title="Sickle cell disease">sickle cell disease</a> in human patients using CRISPR-Cas9 technology have shown promising results.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In December 2023, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">US Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) approved the first cell-based gene therapies for treating sickle cell disease, <a href="/wiki/Exagamglogene_autotemcel" title="Exagamglogene autotemcel">Casgevy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lovotibeglogene_autotemcel" title="Lovotibeglogene autotemcel">Lyfgenia</a>. Casgevy is the first FDA approved gene therapy to use the CRISPR-Cas9 technology and works by modifying a patient's hematopoietic <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Nevertheless, there remains a few limitations of the technology's use in <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a>: the relatively high frequency of <a href="/wiki/Off-target_genome_editing" title="Off-target genome editing">off-target effect</a>, the requirement for a <a href="/wiki/Protospacer_adjacent_motif" title="Protospacer adjacent motif">PAM sequence</a> near the target site, <a href="/wiki/P53" title="P53">p53</a> mediated apoptosis by CRISPR-induced <a href="/wiki/Double-strand_breaks" class="mw-redirect" title="Double-strand breaks">double-strand breaks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immunogenicity" title="Immunogenicity">immunogenic toxicity</a> due to the delivery system typically by <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">virus</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cancer">Cancer</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cancer"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR has also found many applications in developing cell-based immunotherapies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first clinical trial involving CRISPR started in 2016. It involved taking immune cells from people with lung cancer, using CRISPR to edit out the gene which expressed <a href="/wiki/Programmed_cell_death_protein_1" title="Programmed cell death protein 1">Programmed cell death protein 1</a> (PD-1), then administering the altered cells back to the same person. 20 other trials were under way or nearly ready, mostly in China, as of 2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Immuno-Oncology&#95;News&#95;2018&#95;167-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Immuno-Oncology_News_2018-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">United States Food and Drug Administration</a> (FDA) approved a clinical trial in which CRISPR would be used to alter <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a> extracted from people with different kinds of cancer and then administer those engineered T cells back to the same people.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2020, in mouse animal models, CRISPR was used effectively to treat <a href="/wiki/Glioblastoma" title="Glioblastoma">glioblastoma</a> (fast-growing brain tumor) and <a href="/wiki/Metastatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Metastatic">metastatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_cancer" title="Ovarian cancer">ovarian cancer</a>, as those are two cancers with some of the worst best-case prognosis and are typically diagnosed during their later stages. The treatments have resulted in inhibited tumor growth, and increased survival by 80% for metastatic ovarian cancer and tumor cell <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>, inhibited tumor growth by 50%, and improved survival by 30% for glioblastoma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In October 2021, CRISPR Therapeutics announced results from their ongoing US-based phase 1 trial for an allogeneic T cell therapy. These cells are sourced from healthy donors and are edited to attack cancer cells and avoid being seen as a threat by the recipient's immune system, and then multiplied into huge batches which can be given to large numbers of recipients.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Innovative&#95;Genomics&#95;Institute&#95;(IGI)&#95;2022&#95;188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Innovative_Genomics_Institute_(IGI)_2022-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2022, a 13-year British girl that had been diagnosed with incurable <a href="/wiki/T-cell_acute_lymphoblastic_leukemia" title="T-cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia">T-cell acute lymphoblastic leukemia</a> was cured by doctors at <a href="/wiki/Great_Ormond_Street_Hospital" title="Great Ormond Street Hospital">Great Ormond Street Hospital</a>, in the first documented use of <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">therapeutic gene editing</a> for this purpose, after undergoing six months of an experimental treatment, where previous attempts of other treatments failed. The procedure included reprogramming a healthy T-cell to destroy the cancerous T-cells to first rid her of leukaemia, and then rebuilding her immune system from scratch using healthy immune cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The team used <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing#Editing_by_Nucleobase_Modification_(BASE_Editing)" title="Genome editing">BASE editing</a> and had <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy#2015" title="Gene therapy">previously</a> treated a case of <a href="/wiki/Acute_lymphoblastic_leukaemia" class="mw-redirect" title="Acute lymphoblastic leukaemia">acute lymphoblastic leukaemia</a> in 2015 using <a href="/wiki/TALENs" class="mw-redirect" title="TALENs">TALENs</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Diabetes">Diabetes</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Diabetes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Type 1 diabetes is an endocrine disorder which results from a lack of pancreatic beta cells to produce insulin, a vital compound in transporting blood sugar to cells for producing energy. Researchers have been trying to transplant healthy beta cells. CRISPR is used to edit the cells in order to reduce the chance the patient's body will reject the transplant.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>On November 17, 2021 CRISPR therapeutics and ViaCyte announced that the Canadian medical agency had approved their request for a clinical trial for VCTX210, a CRISPR-edited stem cell therapy designed to treat type 1 diabetes. This was significant because it was the first ever gene-edited therapy for diabetes that approached clinics. The same companies also developed a novel treatment for type 1 diabetes to produce insulin via a small medical implant that uses millions of pancreatic cells derived from CRISPR gene-edited stem cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2022, a phase 1 trial was conducted in which one patient volunteer received treatment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Innovative&#95;Genomics&#95;Institute&#95;(IGI)&#95;2022&#95;188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Innovative_Genomics_Institute_(IGI)_2022-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="HIV/AIDS"><span id="HIV.2FAIDS"></span>HIV/AIDS</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: HIV/AIDS"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human_immunodeficiency_virus" class="mw-redirect" title="Human immunodeficiency virus">Human immunodeficiency virus</a> (HIV) is a virus that attacks the body's immune system. While effective treatments exist which can allow patients to live healthy lives, HIV is retroactive meaning that it embeds an inactive version of itself in the human genome. CRISPR can be used to selectively remove the virus from the genome by designing guide RNA to target the retroactive HIV genome. One issue with this approach is that it requires the removal of the HIV genome from almost all cells, which can be difficult to realistically achieve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Innovative&#95;Genomics&#95;Institute&#95;(IGI)&#95;2022&#95;188-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Innovative_Genomics_Institute_(IGI)_2022-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Initial results in the treatment and cure of HIV have been rather successful, in 2021 9 out of 23 humanized mice treated with a combination of anti-retrovirals and CRISPR/Cas-9 had the virus become undetectable, even after the usual rebound period. None of the two treatments alone had such an effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clinical trials in humans of a CRISPR–Cas9 based therapy, <a href="/wiki/Excision_BioTherapeutics" title="Excision BioTherapeutics">EBT-101</a> started in 2022.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In October 2023 an early-stage study on 3 people of EBT-101 reported that the treatment appeared to be safe with no major side effects but no data on its effectiveness was disclosed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In March 2024 another CRISPR therapy from researchers of the university of Amsterdam reported the elimination of HIV in cell cultures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Infection">Infection</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Infection"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR-Cas-based "RNA-guided nucleases" can be used to target <a href="/wiki/Virulence_factors" class="mw-redirect" title="Virulence factors">virulence factors</a>, genes encoding <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a>, and other medically relevant sequences of interest. This technology thus represents a novel form of antimicrobial therapy and a strategy by which to manipulate bacterial populations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gomaaetal2014&#95;199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gomaaetal2014-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-CitorikMimee2014&#95;200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CitorikMimee2014-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent studies suggest a correlation between the interfering of the CRISPR-Cas locus and acquisition of antibiotic resistance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gholizadeh2017&#95;201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gholizadeh2017-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This system provides protection of bacteria against invading foreign DNA, such as <a href="/wiki/Transposon" class="mw-redirect" title="Transposon">transposons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bacteriophage" title="Bacteriophage">bacteriophages</a>, and plasmids. This system was shown to be a strong selective pressure for the acquisition of antibiotic resistance and virulence factor in bacterial pathogens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gholizadeh2017&#95;201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gholizadeh2017-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Therapies based on <a href="/wiki/CRISPR#Cas_genes_and_CRISPR_subtypes" title="CRISPR">CRISPR–Cas3 gene editing</a> technology delivered by engineered bacteriophages could be used to destroy targeted DNA in pathogens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nature&#95;202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cas3 is more destructive than the better known Cas9.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-JNJ&#95;203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JNJ-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nature2&#95;204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature2-204"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Research suggests that CRISPR is an effective way to limit replication of multiple <a href="/wiki/Herpesviridae" title="Herpesviridae">herpesviruses</a>. It was able to eradicate viral DNA in the case of <a href="/wiki/Epstein%E2%80%93Barr_virus" title="Epstein–Barr virus">Epstein–Barr virus</a> (EBV). Anti-herpesvirus CRISPRs have promising applications such as removing cancer-causing EBV from tumor cells, helping rid donated organs for <a href="/wiki/Immunocompromised" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunocompromised">immunocompromised</a> patients of viral invaders, or preventing <a href="/wiki/Herpes_labialis" class="mw-redirect" title="Herpes labialis">cold sore</a> outbreaks and recurrent eye infections by blocking <a href="/wiki/HSV-1" class="mw-redirect" title="HSV-1">HSV-1</a> reactivation. As of August&#160;2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, these were awaiting testing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid27362483&#95;205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid27362483-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR may revive the concept of <a href="/wiki/Xenotransplantation" title="Xenotransplantation">transplanting</a> animal organs into people. <a href="/wiki/Retrovirus" title="Retrovirus">Retroviruses</a> present in animal genomes could harm transplant recipients. In 2015, a team eliminated 62 copies of a particular retroviral DNA sequence from the pig genome in a kidney epithelial cell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Science&#95;Breakthrough&#95;206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Breakthrough-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers recently demonstrated the ability to birth live pig specimens after removing these retroviruses from their genome using CRISPR for the first time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Neurological_disorders">Neurological disorders</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Neurological disorders"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR can be used to suppress gain of function mutations and to repair loss of function mutations in neurological disorders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The gene editing tool has become a foothold in vivo application for assimilation of molecular pathways.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>CRISPR is unique to the development of solving neurological diseases for several reasons. For example, CRISPR allows researchers to quickly generate animal and human cell models, allowing them to study how genes function in a nervous system. By introducing mutations that pertain to various diseases within these cells, researchers can study the effects of the changes on nervous system development, function, and behavior.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They can uncover the molecular mechanisms that contribute to these disorders, which is essential for developing effective treatments. This is particularly useful in modeling and treating complex neurological disorders such as <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer&#39;s disease">Alzheimer's</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson&#39;s disease">Parkinson's</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> among others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Alzheimer's Disease (AD) is a <a href="/wiki/Neurodegenerative_disease" title="Neurodegenerative disease">neurodegenerative disease</a> categorized by neuron loss and an accumulation of intracellular neurofibrillary tangles and extracellular amyloid plaques in the brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-De&#95;Plano&#95;2022&#95;210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-De_Plano_2022-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Three pathogenic genes that cause early onset AD in humans have been identified, specifically amyloid precursor protein (APP), presenilin 1 (PSEN1), and presenilin 2 (PSEN2).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-De&#95;Plano&#95;2022&#95;210-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-De_Plano_2022-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Over 300 mutations have been detected in these genes, resulting in an increase in total β-amyloid (Aβ), Aβ42/40 ratio, and/or Aβ polymerization.
</p><p>CRISPR has been used to correct for the <a href="/wiki/Dystrophin" title="Dystrophin">dystrophin</a> gene, which is responsible for <a href="/wiki/Duchenne_muscular_dystrophy" title="Duchenne muscular dystrophy">Duchenne muscular dystrophy</a>, and for the <a href="/wiki/SCN1A" title="SCN1A">SCN1A</a> mutation responsible for the epilepsy disorder <a href="/wiki/Dravet_syndrome" title="Dravet syndrome">Dravet syndrome</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A challenge of using CRISPR for neurological treatment is transferring its components across the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood-brain barrier</a>. However, recent advancements in nanoparticle delivery systems and viral vectors have shown promise in overcoming this hurdle<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. Looking to the future, the use of CRISPR in neuroscience is expected to increase as technology evolves.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Blindness">Blindness</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Blindness"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The most commonly occurring worldwide eye diseases are <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataract</a> and <a href="/wiki/Retinitis_pigmentosa" title="Retinitis pigmentosa">retinitis pigmentosa</a> (RP). These are caused by a missense mutation in the alpha chain that leads to permanent blindness. A challenge to the use of CRISPR 77hh in eye disease is that retinal tissue in the eye is free from body immune response. Researchers' approach for using CRISPR is to bag the gene coding retinal protein and edit the genome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Leber_congenital_amaurosis">Leber congenital amaurosis</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Leber congenital amaurosis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The CRISPR treatment for LCA10 (the most common variant of <a href="/wiki/Leber_congenital_amaurosis" title="Leber congenital amaurosis">Leber congenital amaurosis</a> which is the leading cause of inherited childhood blindness) modifies the patient's defective photoreceptor gene.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In March 2020, the first patient volunteer in this US-based study, sponsored by <a href="/wiki/Editas_Medicine" title="Editas Medicine">Editas Medicine</a>, was given a low-dose of the treatment to test for safety. In June 2021, enrollment began for a high-dose adult and pediatric cohort of 4 patient volunteers each. Dosing of the new cohorts was expected to be completed by July 2022.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Innovative&#95;Genomics&#95;Institute&#95;(IGI)&#95;2022&#95;188-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Innovative_Genomics_Institute_(IGI)_2022-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In November 2022, Editas reported that 20% of the patients treated had significant improvements, but also announced that the resulting target population was too small to support continued independent development.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Cardiovascular_diseases">Cardiovascular diseases</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Cardiovascular diseases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR technology has been shown to work efficiently in the treatment of heart disease. In the case of <a href="/wiki/Familial_hypercholesterolemia" title="Familial hypercholesterolemia">familial hypercholesterolemia</a> (FH), cholesterol deposition in the walls of the artery causes blockage of blood flow. This is caused by mutation in low density lipoprotein cholesterol receptors <a href="/wiki/Low-density_lipoprotein" title="Low-density lipoprotein">(LDLC)</a> which results in excessive release of cholesterol into the blood. This can be treated by deletion of a base pair in exon 4 of the LDLC receptor. This is a <a href="/wiki/Nonsense_mutation" title="Nonsense mutation">nonsense mutation</a>. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="β-Hemoglobinopathies"><span id=".CE.B2-Hemoglobinopathies"></span>β-Hemoglobinopathies</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: β-Hemoglobinopathies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>This disease comes under genetic disorders which are caused by mutation occurring in the structure of <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> or due to substitution of different amino acids in globin chains. Due to this, the red blood cells (RBC) cause a string of obstacles such as heart failure, hindrance of blood vessels, defects in growth and optical problems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To rehabilitate β-hemoglobinopathies, the patient's multipotent cells are transferred in a mice model to study the rate of gene therapy in ex-vivo which results in expression of mRNA and the gene being rectified. Intriguingly RBC half-life was also increased.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hemophilia">Hemophilia</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Hemophilia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Haemophilia" title="Haemophilia">Hemophilia</a> is a loss of the clotting function in blood due to one of the clotting proteins not working properly. A research team created a line of mice that had the same defective clotting protein found in human <a href="/wiki/Hemophilia_B" class="mw-redirect" title="Hemophilia B">Hemophilia B </a>. By using CRISPR-Cas9 the team was able to insert a gene for a working version of the defective protein into the liver cells of these mice. Enough liver cells were repaired to alleviate the symptoms of heamophilia in these mice.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Agriculture">Agriculture</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Agriculture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Successful CRISPR-Cas9 genome editing was first achieved in plants in August 2013.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has since been successfully applied in several key crop species for the purpose of introducing or improving numerous agricultural traits.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zhu&#95;2020&#95;219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu_2020-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The development of CRISPR technology has been highly influential in the field of plant biotechnology, and has the potential to revolutionize the future of agriculture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zhu&#95;2020&#95;219-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu_2020-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR-Cas systems are most commonly introduced into plants by <a href="/wiki/Agrobacterium_tumefaciens" title="Agrobacterium tumefaciens">Agrobacterium</a>-mediated transformation, although <a href="/wiki/Gene_gun" title="Gene gun">particle bombardment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protoplast" title="Protoplast">protoplast</a> transformation are also used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Yield">Yield</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Yield"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Improvement of crop yields has been achieved in several species through the use of CRISPR-Cas technology. Grain yield in cereal crops is influenced by levels of the plant hormone <a href="/wiki/Cytokinin" title="Cytokinin">cytokinin</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> High-yielding rice<sup id="cite&#95;ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and wheat<sup id="cite&#95;ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> varieties have been produced by using CRISPR-Cas9 to knock out the enzyme <a href="/wiki/Cytokinin_dehydrogenase" title="Cytokinin dehydrogenase">cytokinin oxidase/dehydrogenase</a> (CKX), which degrades cytokinin. Grain yield has also been increased in rice by using CRISPR-Cas9 to knock out an <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid_transporter" title="Amino acid transporter">amino acid transporter</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Quality">Quality</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Quality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR has been used to develop higher quality crops, including improvements to physical appearance, flavor and aroma, texture, shelf life, and nutritional content.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pink,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> yellow,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>227<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and purple<sup id="cite&#95;ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>228<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> tomatoes have been produced by using CRISPR to mutate genes involved in synthesizing pigments. CRISPR has also been used to decrease <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> content in wheat, thus improving grain quality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>229<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, soybeans have been modified using CRISPR to contain more heart-healthy monounsaturated fatty acids, like <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR technology has also been used to reduce the amount of <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergens</a> in foods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chakraborty&#95;2024&#95;231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chakraborty_2024-231"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Wheat containing decreased levels of <a href="/wiki/Gluten" title="Gluten">gluten</a>, a common allergen and <a href="/wiki/Coeliac_disease" title="Coeliac disease">intolerance</a>, has been developed using CRISPR.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>232<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers are also working to reduce allergens in soybean, peanut, and mustard using CRISPR-Cas9.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chakraborty&#95;2024&#95;231-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chakraborty_2024-231"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Resistance_to_disease">Resistance to disease</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Resistance to disease"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>CRISPR has been used to develop plants with improved <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">resistance</a> to various diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Borrelli&#95;2018&#95;233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borrelli_2018-233"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Using CRISPR, cucumber, rice, and tobacco plants have been engineered with resistance to <a href="/wiki/Plant_virus" title="Plant virus">viruses</a>. Wheat, rice, tomato, grape, and cacao have been modified for resistance to <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease#Fungi" title="Plant disease">fungal diseases</a>. Finally, rice, apple, and citrus fruits have been developed with resistance to <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease#Bacteria" title="Plant disease">bacterial infection</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Borrelli&#95;2018&#95;233-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borrelli_2018-233"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Gene_therapy">Gene therapy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Gene therapy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are currently about 6000 known genetic disorders, most of which are currently untreatable. The role of CRISPR in gene therapy is to substitute exogenous DNA in place of defective genes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>234<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Gene therapy has made a huge impact and opened many new possibilities in medical biotechnology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Base_editing">Base editing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Base editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>There are two types of base editings:
</p><p>Cytidine base editor is a novel therapy in which the cytidine (C) changes to thymidine (T).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Adenine base editor (ABE),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>235<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in this there is a change in base complements from adenine (A) to Guanine (G).
</p><p>The mutations were directly installed in cellular DNA so that the donor template is not required. The base editings can only edit point mutations. Moreover, they can only fix up to four-point mutations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To address this problem, the CRISPR system introduced a new technique known as <a href="/wiki/Cas9" title="Cas9">Cas9</a> fusion to increase the scale of genes that can be edited.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Gene_silencing_and_activating">Gene silencing and activating</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Gene silencing and activating"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Furthermore, the CRISPR Cas9 protein can modulate genes either by activating or silencing based on genes of interest.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>237<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is a nuclease called dCas9 (endonuclease) used to silence or activate the expression of genes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Limitations">Limitations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Limitations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The researchers are facing many challenges in gene editing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>238<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The major hurdles coming in the clinical applications are ethical issues and the transport system to the target site. As the units of CRISPR system taken from bacteria, when they are transferred to host cells it produces an immune response against them. Physical, chemical, viral vectors are used as vehicles to deliver the complex into the host.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Due to this many complications are arising such as cell damage that leads to cell death. In the case of viral vectors, the capacity of the virus is small and Cas9 protein is large. So, to overcome these new methods were developed in which smaller strains of Cas9 are taken from bacteria. Finally, a great extent of work is still needed to improve the system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="As_a_diagnostic_tool">As a diagnostic tool</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: As a diagnostic tool"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:CRISPR_diagnostics_diagram.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/CRISPR_diagnostics_diagram.svg/250px-CRISPR_diagnostics_diagram.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/CRISPR_diagnostics_diagram.svg/500px-CRISPR_diagnostics_diagram.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="288" /></a><figcaption>Simplified flowchart for CRISPR based diagnostics like SHERLOCK<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nucleic&#95;acid&#95;detection&#95;with&#95;CRISPR&#95;239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nucleic_acid_detection_with_CRISPR-239"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:F2._CRISPR.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/F2._CRISPR.jpg/250px-F2._CRISPR.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="233" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/F2._CRISPR.jpg/500px-F2._CRISPR.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2999" data-file-height="2793" /></a><figcaption>Schematic flowchart of molecular detection methods for COVID-19 virus<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dhamad&#95;2020&#95;240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhamad_2020-240"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>CRISPR associated nucleases have shown to be useful as a tool for molecular testing due to their ability to specifically target nucleic acid sequences in a high background of non-target sequences. In 2016, the Cas9 nuclease was used to deplete unwanted nucleotide sequences in next-generation sequencing libraries while requiring only 250 picograms of initial RNA input.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>241<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Beginning in 2017, CRISPR associated nucleases were also used for direct diagnostic testing of nucleic acids, down to single molecule sensitivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nucleic&#95;acid&#95;detection&#95;with&#95;CRISPR&#95;239-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nucleic_acid_detection_with_CRISPR-239"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>242<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR diversity is used as an analysis target to discern <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> and diversity in bacteria, such as in <a href="/wiki/Xanthomonad" class="mw-redirect" title="Xanthomonad">xanthomonads</a> by Martins <i>et al.</i>, 2019.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Elsalam-Lim-2021&#95;243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elsalam-Lim-2021-243"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>243<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 552">&#58;&#8202;552&#8202;</span></sup> Early detections of <a href="/wiki/Plant_pathogenic_bacteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Plant pathogenic bacteria">plant pathogens</a> by molecular typing of the pathogen's CRISPRs can be used in agriculture as demonstrated by Shen <i>et al.</i>, 2020.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Elsalam-Lim-2021&#95;243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Elsalam-Lim-2021-243"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>243<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 553">&#58;&#8202;553&#8202;</span></sup>
</p><p>By coupling CRISPR-based diagnostics to additional enzymatic processes, the detection of molecules beyond nucleic acids is possible. One example of a coupled technology is SHERLOCK-based Profiling of IN vitro Transcription (SPRINT). SPRINT can be used to detect a variety of substances, such as metabolites in patient samples or contaminants in environmental samples, with high throughput or with portable point-of-care devices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SPRINT:&#95;a&#95;Cas13a-based&#95;platform&#95;for&#95;244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPRINT:_a_Cas13a-based_platform_for-244"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>244<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR-Cas platforms are also being explored for detection<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CRISPR-Cas12-based&#95;detection&#95;of&#95;SAR&#95;245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRISPR-Cas12-based_detection_of_SAR-245"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>245<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Enhancement&#95;of&#95;trans-cleavage&#95;activ&#95;246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enhancement_of_trans-cleavage_activ-246"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>246<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dhamad&#95;2020&#95;240-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhamad_2020-240"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>247<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>248<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and inactivation of <a href="/wiki/SARS-CoV-2" title="SARS-CoV-2">SARS-CoV-2</a>, the virus that causes <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>249<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two different comprehensive diagnostic tests, AIOD-CRISPR and SHERLOCK test have been identified for SARS-CoV-2.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>250<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The SHERLOCK test is based on a fluorescently labelled press reporter RNA which has the ability to identify 10 copies per microliter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>251<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The AIOD-CRISPR helps with robust and highly sensitive visual detection of the viral nucleic acid.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>252<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Genetic_anthropology">Genetic anthropology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Genetic anthropology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_brain#Genetic_factors_of_recent_evolution" title="Evolution of the brain">Evolution of the brain §&#160;Genetic factors of recent evolution</a></div>
<p>CRISPR-Cas9 can be used in investigating and identifying the genetic differences of humans to other apes, especially <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_the_brain#Genetic_factors_of_recent_evolution" title="Evolution of the brain">of the brain</a>. For example, by reintroducing archaic gene variants into <a href="/wiki/Brain_organoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain organoid">brain organoids</a> to show an impact on neurogenesis,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>253<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> metaphase length of apical progenitors of the developing neocortex,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>254<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or by knockout of a gene in embryonic stem cells to identify a genetic regulator that via early cell shape transition drives <a href="/wiki/Brain_size" title="Brain size">evolutionary expansion of the human forebrain</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>255<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1016/j.cell.2021.02.050&#95;256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.cell.2021.02.050-256"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>256<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One study described a major impact of an archaic gene variant on <a href="/wiki/Neurodevelopment" class="mw-redirect" title="Neurodevelopment">neurodevelopment</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>257<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>258<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which may be an artefact of a CRISPR side effect,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>259<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>260<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as it could not be replicated in a subsequent study.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceB&#95;85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="By_technique">By technique</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: By technique"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Knockdown/activation"><span id="Knockdown.2Factivation"></span>Knockdown/activation</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Knockdown/activation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/CRISPR_interference" title="CRISPR interference">CRISPR interference</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dead-Cas9_potential_applications.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Dead-Cas9_potential_applications.png/500px-Dead-Cas9_potential_applications.png" decoding="async" width="380" height="529" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Dead-Cas9_potential_applications.png/960px-Dead-Cas9_potential_applications.png 2x" data-file-width="2730" data-file-height="3801" /></a><figcaption>A dead Cas9 protein coupled with epigenetic modifiers which are used to repress certain genome sequences rather than cutting it all together<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ledford&#95;2016&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ledford_2016-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Using "dead" versions of Cas9 (<a href="/wiki/Cas9#Interference_of_transcription_by_dCas9" title="Cas9">dCas9</a>) eliminates CRISPR's DNA-cutting ability, while preserving its ability to target desirable sequences. Multiple groups added various regulatory factors to dCas9s, enabling them to turn almost any gene on or off or adjust its level of activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Science&#95;Breakthrough&#95;206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Breakthrough-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like RNAi, CRISPR interference (CRISPRi) turns off genes in a reversible fashion by targeting, but not cutting a site. The targeted site is methylated, <a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetically</a> modifying the gene. This modification inhibits transcription. These precisely placed modifications may then be used to regulate the effects on gene expressions and DNA dynamics after the inhibition of certain genome sequences within DNA. Within the past few years, epigenetic marks in different human cells have been closely researched and certain patterns within the marks have been found to correlate with everything ranging from tumor growth to brain activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ledford&#95;2016&#95;10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ledford_2016-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Conversely, CRISPR-mediated activation (CRISPRa) promotes gene transcription.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>261<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cas9 is an effective way of targeting and silencing specific genes at the DNA level.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid24336571&#95;262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24336571-262"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>262<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In bacteria, the presence of Cas9 alone is enough to block transcription. For mammalian applications, a section of protein is added. Its guide RNA targets regulatory DNA sequences called <a href="/wiki/Promoter_(genetics)" title="Promoter (genetics)">promoters</a> that immediately precede the target gene.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-craze2&#95;263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-craze2-263"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>263<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Cas9 was used to carry synthetic <a href="/wiki/Transcription_factor" title="Transcription factor">transcription factors</a> that activated specific human genes. The technique achieved a strong effect by targeting multiple CRISPR constructs to slightly different locations on the gene's promoter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-craze2&#95;263-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-craze2-263"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>263<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="RNA_editing">RNA editing</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: RNA editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/RNA_editing" title="RNA editing">RNA editing</a></div>
<p>In 2016, researchers demonstrated that CRISPR from an ordinary mouth bacterium could be used to edit <a href="/wiki/RNA" title="RNA">RNA</a>. The researchers searched databases containing hundreds of millions of genetic sequences for those that resembled CRISPR genes. They considered the fusobacterium <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Leptotrichia_shahii&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Leptotrichia shahii (page does not exist)">Leptotrichia shahii</a></i>. It had a group of genes that resembled CRISPR genes, but with important differences. When the researchers equipped other bacteria with these genes, which they called C2c2, they found that the organisms gained a novel defense.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zimmer&#95;2016&#95;264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2016-264"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>264<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> C2c2 has later been renamed to Cas13a to fit the standard nomenclature for Cas genes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>265<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many viruses encode their genetic information in RNA rather than DNA that they repurpose to make new viruses. <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poliovirus" title="Poliovirus">poliovirus</a> are such viruses. Bacteria with Cas13 make molecules that can dismember RNA, destroying the virus. Tailoring these genes opened any RNA molecule to editing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zimmer&#95;2016&#95;264-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2016-264"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>264<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>CRISPR-Cas systems can also be employed for editing of <a href="/wiki/MicroRNA" title="MicroRNA">micro-RNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long_non-coding_RNA" title="Long non-coding RNA">long-noncoding RNA</a> genes in plants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>266<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Therapeutic_applications">Therapeutic applications</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Therapeutic applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/RNA_editing#Therapeutic_mRNA_editing" title="RNA editing">RNA editing § Therapeutic mRNA editing</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=RNA_editing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Directing edits to correct mutated sequences was first proposed and demonstrated in 1995.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RNA&#95;editing&#95;Woolf&#95;1995&#95;267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNA_editing_Woolf_1995-267"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>267<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  This initial work used synthetic RNA antisense oligonucleotides complementary to a pre-mature stop codon mutation in a dystrophin sequence to activate A-to-I editing of the stop codon to a read through codon in a model xenopus cell system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RNA&#95;editing&#95;Woolf&#95;1995&#95;267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RNA_editing_Woolf_1995-267"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>267<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  While this also led to nearby inadvertent A-to-I transitions, A to I (read as G) transitions can correct all three stop codons, but cannot create a stop codon.  Therefore, the changes led &gt;25% correction of the targeted stop codon with read through to a downstream luciferase reporter sequence.  Follow on work by Rosenthal achieved editing of mutated mRNA sequence in mammalian cell culture by directing an oligonucleotide linked to a cytidine deaminase to correct a mutated cystic fibrosis sequence.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>268<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  More recently, CRISPR-Cas13 fused to deaminases has been employed to direct mRNA editing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>269<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2022, therapeutic RNA editing for Cas7-11 was reported.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>270<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>271<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It enables sufficiently targeted cuts and an early version of it was used for <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i> editing in 2021.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>272<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Comparison_to_DNA_editing">Comparison to DNA editing</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Comparison to DNA editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/RNA_editing#Comparison_to_DNA_editing" title="RNA editing">RNA editing § Comparison to DNA editing</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=RNA_editing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Unlike DNA editing, which is permanent, the effects of RNA editing − including potential <a href="/wiki/Off-target_genome_editing" title="Off-target genome editing">off-target mutations</a> in RNA − are transient and are not inherited. RNA editing is therefore considered to be less risky. Furthermore, it may only require a guide RNA by using the ADAR protein already found in humans and many other eukaryotes' cells instead of needing to introduce a foreign protein into the body.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>273<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Gene_drive">Gene drive</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Gene drive"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gene_drive" title="Gene drive">Gene drive</a></div>
<p>Gene drives may provide a powerful tool to restore balance of ecosystems by eliminating invasive species. Concerns regarding efficacy, unintended consequences in the target species as well as non-target species have been raised particularly in the potential for accidental release from laboratories into the wild. Scientists have proposed several safeguards for ensuring the containment of experimental gene drives including molecular, reproductive, and ecological.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>274<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many recommend that immunization and reversal drives be developed in tandem with gene drives in order to overwrite their effects if necessary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>275<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There remains consensus that long-term effects must be studied more thoroughly particularly in the potential for ecological disruption that cannot be corrected with reversal drives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>276<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
