<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="In_vitro_genetic_depletion"><i>In vitro</i> genetic depletion</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: In vitro genetic depletion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Unenriched sequencing libraries often have abundant undesired sequences. Cas9 can specifically deplete the undesired sequences with double strand breakage with up to 99% efficiency and without significant <a href="/wiki/Off-target_genome_editing" title="Off-target genome editing">off-target effects</a> as seen with <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzymes</a>. Treatment with Cas9 can deplete abundant rRNA while increasing pathogen sensitivity in RNA-seq libraries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid26944702&#95;277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26944702-277"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>277<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Epigenome_editing">Epigenome editing</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Epigenome editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Epigenome_editing" title="Epigenome editing">Epigenome editing</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Epigenome_editing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Epigenome_editing.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Epigenome_editing.png/330px-Epigenome_editing.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="268" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e5/Epigenome_editing.png 2x" data-file-width="950" data-file-height="850" /></a><figcaption>A visual overview of how TALE proteins are used for epigenome editing</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Epigenome_editing" title="Epigenome editing">Epigenome editing</a> or epigenome engineering is a type of genetic engineering in which the <a href="/wiki/Epigenome" title="Epigenome">epigenome</a> is modified at specific sites using engineered molecules targeted to those sites (as opposed to whole-genome modifications). Whereas gene editing involves changing the actual DNA sequence itself, epigenetic editing involves modifying and presenting DNA sequences to proteins and other DNA binding factors that influence DNA function. By "editing" epigenomic features in this manner, researchers can determine the exact biological role of an epigenetic modification at the site in question.
</p><p>The engineered proteins used for epigenome editing are composed of a DNA binding domain that targets specific sequences and an effector domain that modifies epigenomic features. Currently, three major groups of DNA binding proteins have been predominantly used for epigenome editing: <a href="/wiki/Zinc_finger" title="Zinc finger">Zinc finger</a> proteins, <a href="/wiki/TAL_effector" class="mw-redirect" title="TAL effector">Transcription Activator-Like Effectors (TALEs)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclease" title="Nuclease">nuclease</a> deficient Cas9 fusions (<a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a>).
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Applications_2">Applications</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Applications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Epigenome_editing#Functional_engineering" title="Epigenome editing">Epigenome editing § Functional engineering</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Epigenome_editing&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p>Targeted regulation of disease-related genes may enable novel therapies for many diseases, especially in cases where adequate gene therapies are not yet developed or are inappropriate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1016/j.tig.2015.12.001&#95;278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.tig.2015.12.001-278"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>278<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While transgenerational and population level consequences are not fully understood, it may become a major tool for applied <a href="/wiki/Functional_genomics" title="Functional genomics">functional genomics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Personalized_medicine" title="Personalized medicine">personalized medicine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>279<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As with <a href="/wiki/RNA_editing" title="RNA editing">RNA editing</a>, it does not involve genetic changes and their accompanying risks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10.1016/j.tig.2015.12.001&#95;278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.tig.2015.12.001-278"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>278<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One example of a potential functional use of epigenome editing was described in 2021: repressing <a href="/wiki/Nav1.7" class="mw-redirect" title="Nav1.7">Na<sub>v</sub>1.7</a> <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> via <a href="/wiki/CRISPR_activation#dCas9" title="CRISPR activation">CRISPR-dCas9</a> which showed therapeutic potential in three mouse models of chronic pain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>280<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>281<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2022, research assessed its usefulness in reducing <a href="/wiki/Tau_protein" title="Tau protein">tau protein</a> levels, regulating a protein involved in <a href="/wiki/Huntington%27s_disease" title="Huntington&#39;s disease">Huntington's disease</a>, targeting an inherited form of obesity, and <a href="/wiki/Dravet_syndrome" title="Dravet syndrome">Dravet syndrome</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>282<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2024, a study about diabetic<sup id="cite&#95;ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>283<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wound_healing" title="Wound healing">wound healing</a> showed that the modification of <a href="/wiki/Histone" title="Histone">histones</a> through <a href="/wiki/Methylation" title="Methylation">metylation</a> could stimulate the productions of large-scale EVs (<a href="/wiki/Extracellular_vesicle" title="Extracellular vesicle">Extracellular vesicles</a>) to reduce <a href="/wiki/Angiogenesis" title="Angiogenesis">angiogenesis</a> for diabetic foot ulcers, thus reducing death danger.
</p>
</div></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="CRISPR-directed_integrases">CRISPR-directed integrases</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: CRISPR-directed integrases"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Combination of CRISPR-Cas9 with <a href="/wiki/Integrase" title="Integrase">integrases</a> enabled a technique for <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1038841319" /><span class="rt-commentedText tooltip tooltip-dotted" title="&quot;allowing large, multiplexed gene insertion without reliance on DNA repair pathways&quot;">large edits</span> without problematic double-stranded breaks, as demonstrated with <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1038841319" /><span class="rt-commentedText tooltip tooltip-dotted" title="Programmable Addition via Site-specific Targeting Elements">PASTE</span> in 2022. The researchers reported it could be used to deliver genes as long as 36,000 DNA <a href="/wiki/Base_pair" title="Base pair">base pairs</a> to several types of human cells and thereby potentially for treating diseases caused by a large number of mutations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>284<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>285<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Prime_editing">Prime editing</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Prime editing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prime_editing" title="Prime editing">Prime editing</a></div>
<p>Prime editing<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nature&#95;Oct2019&#95;286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nature_Oct2019-286"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>286<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a CRISPR refinement to precisely replace, insert, or delete sections of DNA. CRISPR edits are generally inefficient, fairly error-prone, and can lack target specificity, which are significant problems for using the technology in medicine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thulin&#95;Oct2019&#95;287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thulin_Oct2019-287"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>287<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Prime editing cuts a single strand of targeted genomic DNA using a CRISPR nickase, then uses a tethered reverse transcriptase to add a desired sequence onto the cut DNA end that can be incorporated into the genome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cohen&#95;Oct2019&#95;288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen_Oct2019-288"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>288<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An associated guide RNA, called a pegRNA, contains both a sequence for determining the targeted genetic sequence and a template for the new DNA sequence to be incorporated into the genome.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yasinski&#95;Oct2019&#95;289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yasinski_Oct2019-289"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>289<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This mechanism for installing genome edits can be both highly efficient and precise, with low rates of unintended sequence incorporation errors like indels. The independent pairing events for the target and template together help prevent off-target edits, which significantly increases targeting flexibility and editing specificity. Prime editing was developed by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Broad_Institute" title="Broad Institute">Broad Institute of MIT and Harvard</a> in Massachusetts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BBC&#95;Oct2019&#95;290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Oct2019-290"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>290<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Society and culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Human_germline_modification">Human germline modification</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Human germline modification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As of March 2015, multiple groups had announced ongoing research with the intention of laying the foundations for applying CRISPR to human embryos for <a href="/wiki/Human_germline_engineering" title="Human germline engineering">human germline engineering</a>, including labs in the US, China, and the UK, as well as US biotechnology company <a href="/wiki/OvaScience" title="OvaScience">OvaScience</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>291<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Scientists, including a CRISPR co-discoverer, urged a worldwide moratorium on applying CRISPR to the human germline, especially for clinical use. They said "scientists should avoid even attempting, in lax jurisdictions, germline genome modification for clinical application in humans" until the full implications "are discussed among scientific and governmental organizations".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-SCI-20150319&#95;292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20150319-292"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>292<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NAT-20150312&#95;293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20150312-293"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>293<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These scientists support further low-level research on CRISPR and do not see CRISPR as developed enough for any clinical use in making heritable changes to humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NYT-20150319&#95;294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150319-294"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>294<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2015, Chinese scientists reported results of an attempt to alter the DNA of non-viable <a href="/wiki/Human_embryos" class="mw-redirect" title="Human embryos">human embryos</a> using CRISPR to correct a mutation that causes <a href="/wiki/Beta_thalassemia" title="Beta thalassemia">beta thalassemia</a>, a lethal heritable disorder.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-LiangXu2015&#95;295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LiangXu2015-295"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>295<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NYT-20150423&#95;296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150423-296"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>296<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The study had previously been rejected by both <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> in part because of ethical concerns.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NatureNews&#95;297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatureNews-297"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>297<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The experiments resulted in successfully changing only some of the intended genes, and had <a href="/wiki/Off-target_effects_of_genome_editing" class="mw-redirect" title="Off-target effects of genome editing">off-target effects</a> on other genes. The researchers stated that CRISPR is not ready for clinical application in <a href="/wiki/Reproductive_medicine" title="Reproductive medicine">reproductive medicine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NatureNews&#95;297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NatureNews-297"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>297<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In April 2016, Chinese scientists were reported to have made a second unsuccessful attempt to alter the DNA of non-viable human embryos using CRISPR – this time to alter the <a href="/wiki/CCR5" title="CCR5">CCR5</a> gene to make the embryo resistant to <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> infection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>298<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2015, an International Summit on Human Gene Editing took place in Washington under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/David_Baltimore" title="David Baltimore">David Baltimore</a>. Members of national scientific academies of the US, UK, and China discussed the ethics of germline modification. They agreed to support basic and clinical research under certain legal and ethical guidelines. A specific distinction was made between <a href="/wiki/Somatic_cell" title="Somatic cell">somatic cells</a>, where the effects of edits are limited to a single individual, and germline cells, where genome changes can be inherited by descendants. Heritable modifications could have unintended and far-reaching consequences for human evolution, genetically (e.g. gene–environment interactions) and culturally (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinism</a>). Altering of <a href="/wiki/Gametocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gametocytes">gametocytes</a> and embryos to generate heritable changes in humans was defined to be irresponsible. The group agreed to initiate an international forum to address such concerns and harmonize regulations across countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-National&#95;Academy&#95;of&#95;Science&#95;299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-National_Academy_of_Science-299"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>299<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2017, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">United States National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine</a> (<a href="/wiki/NASEM" class="mw-redirect" title="NASEM">NASEM</a>) Committee on Human Gene Editing published a report reviewing ethical, legal, and scientific concerns of genomic engineering technology. The conclusion of the report stated that heritable <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing" title="Genome editing">genome editing</a> is impermissible now but could be justified for certain medical conditions; however, they did not justify the usage of <a href="/wiki/CRISPR" title="CRISPR">CRISPR</a> for enhancement.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>300<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2018, <a href="/wiki/He_Jiankui" title="He Jiankui">Jiankui He</a> announced that he had edited two human embryos to attempt to disable the gene for <a href="/wiki/CCR5" title="CCR5">CCR5</a>, which codes for a receptor that <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> uses to enter cells. He said that twin girls, <a href="/wiki/Lulu_and_Nana_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Lulu and Nana controversy">Lulu and Nana</a>, had been born a few weeks earlier. He said that the girls still carried functional copies of CCR5 along with disabled CCR5 (<a href="/wiki/Mosaicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosaicism">mosaicism</a>) and were still vulnerable to HIV. The work was widely condemned as unethical, dangerous, and premature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>301<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An international group of scientists called for a global moratorium on genetically editing human embryos.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>302<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Policy_barriers_to_genetic_engineering">Policy barriers to genetic engineering</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Policy barriers to genetic engineering"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Policy regulations for the CRISPR-Cas9 system vary around the globe. In February 2016, British scientists were given permission by regulators to genetically modify <a href="/wiki/Human_embryo" class="mw-redirect" title="Human embryo">human embryos</a> by using CRISPR-Cas9 and related techniques. However, researchers were forbidden from implanting the embryos and the embryos were to be destroyed after seven days.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>303<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The US has an elaborate, interdepartmental regulatory system to evaluate new genetically modified foods and crops. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture_Risk_Protection_Act_of_2000" title="Agriculture Risk Protection Act of 2000">Agriculture Risk Protection Act of 2000</a> gives the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">United States Department of Agriculture</a> the authority to oversee the detection, control, eradication, suppression, prevention, or retardation of the spread of plant pests or noxious weeds to protect the agriculture, environment, and economy of the US. The act regulates any <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organism</a> that utilizes the genome of a predefined "plant pest" or any plant not previously categorized.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>304<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2015, Yinong Yang successfully deactivated 16 specific genes in the <a href="/wiki/White_button_mushroom" class="mw-redirect" title="White button mushroom">white button mushroom</a> to make them non-browning. Since he had not added any foreign-species (<a href="/wiki/Transgenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic">transgenic</a>) DNA to his organism, the mushroom could not be regulated by the USDA under Section 340.2.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>305<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Yang's white button mushroom was the first organism genetically modified with the CRISPR-Cas9 protein system to pass US regulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Waltz&#95;2016&#95;306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waltz_2016-306"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>306<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, the USDA sponsored a committee to consider future regulatory policy for upcoming genetic modification techniques. With the help of the US <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine</a>, special interests groups met on April 15 to contemplate the possible advancements in genetic engineering within the next five years and any new regulations that might be needed as a result.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>307<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> proposed a rule that would classify genetic engineering modifications to animals as "animal drugs", subjecting them to strict regulation if offered for sale and reducing the ability for individuals and small businesses to make them profitable.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>308<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>309<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In China, where social conditions sharply contrast with those of the West, genetic diseases carry a heavy stigma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cyranoski&#95;2017&#95;310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cyranoski_2017-310"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>310<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This leaves China with fewer policy barriers to the use of this technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peng&#95;2016&#95;311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peng_2016-311"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>311<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-WSJ&#95;2018&#95;312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_2018-312"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>312<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Recognition">Recognition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=CRISPR_gene_editing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Recognition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2012 and 2013, CRISPR was a runner-up in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Science Magazine">Science Magazine</a></i>'s <a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_of_the_Year" title="Breakthrough of the Year">Breakthrough of the Year</a> award. In 2015, it was the winner of that award.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Science&#95;Breakthrough&#95;206-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_Breakthrough-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> CRISPR was named as one of <i><a href="/wiki/MIT_Technology_Review" title="MIT Technology Review">MIT Technology Review</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s 10 breakthrough technologies in 2014 and 2016.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MITCRISPR&#95;313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MITCRISPR-313"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>313<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-MITGene&#95;314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MITGene-314"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>314<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2016, <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Doudna" title="Jennifer Doudna">Jennifer Doudna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emmanuelle_Charpentier" title="Emmanuelle Charpentier">Emmanuelle Charpentier</a>, along with Rudolph Barrangou, <a href="/wiki/Philippe_Horvath" title="Philippe Horvath">Philippe Horvath</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feng_Zhang" title="Feng Zhang">Feng Zhang</a> won the Gairdner International award. In 2017, Doudna and Charpentier were awarded the Japan Prize in Tokyo, Japan for their revolutionary invention of CRISPR-Cas9. In 2016, Charpentier, Doudna, and Zhang won the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Prize" title="Tang Prize">Tang Prize</a> in Biopharmaceutical Science.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>315<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2020, Charpentier and Doudna were awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a>, "for the development of a method for genome editing."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>316<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite&#95;note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFŠikšnys2018" class="citation episode cs1">Šikšnys V (2018-06-16). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_NQqKMyXSYQ&amp;t=12m22s">"Imam genų žirkles, iškerpam klaidą, ligos nelieka"</a>. <i>Laisvės TV / Freedom TV</i>.  12:22 minutes in. LaisvėsTV. &lt;...&gt;Tai mes tą savo straipsnį išsiuntėm į redakciją pirmieji, bet laimės ten daug nebuvo. Viena redakcija pasakė, kad mes net recenzentam nesiųsim. Nusiuntėm į kitą redakciją – tai jis (straipsnis) pragulėjo kažkur ant redaktoriaus stalo labai ilgai. Na ir taip galų gale išsiuntėm į trečią žurnalą ir trečias žurnalas po kelių mėnesių jį išspausdino. Bet, aišku, Berklio universiteto mokslininkams sekėsi geriau – jie išsiuntė straipsnį į žurnalą Science – jį priėmė ir išspausdino per 2 savaites. Nors iš tikro jie tą straispnį išsiuntė pora mėnesių vėliau nei mes<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2018-06-30</span></span>. <q>&lt;...&gt; Well, we were who had sent the article first, but had not much of luck.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Laisv%C4%97s+TV+%2F+Freedom+TV&amp;rft.date=2018-06-16&amp;rft.aulast=%C5%A0ik%C5%A1nys&amp;rft.aufirst=V&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.youtube.com%2Fwatch%3Fv%3D&#95;NQqKMyXSYQ%26t%3D12m22s&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACRISPR+gene+editing" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>

<ul><li><span class="nowrap">1996: <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> understanding</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">1997: <a href="/wiki/Dolly_(sheep)" title="Dolly (sheep)">Dolly the sheep</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">1998: <a href="/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe" title="Accelerating expansion of the universe">Accelerating universe</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">1999: <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">Stem cell</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2000: <a href="/wiki/Whole_genome_sequencing" title="Whole genome sequencing">Whole genome sequencing</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2001: <a href="/wiki/Nanocircuitry" title="Nanocircuitry">Nanocircuits</a> or <a href="/wiki/Molecular_scale_electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular scale electronics">Molecular circuit</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2002: <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2003: <a href="/wiki/Dark_energy" title="Dark energy">Dark energy</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2004: <a href="/wiki/Spirit_(rover)" title="Spirit (rover)"><i>Spirit</i> rover</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2005: <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolution</a> in action</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2006: <a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_conjecture" title="Poincaré conjecture">Poincaré conjecture</a> proof</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2007: <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_variation" title="Human genetic variation">Human genetic variation</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2008: <a href="/wiki/Induced_pluripotent_stem_cell" title="Induced pluripotent stem cell">Cellular reprogramming</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2009: <i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus_ramidus" title="Ardipithecus ramidus">Ardipithecus ramidus</a></i></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2010: First <a href="/wiki/Quantum_machine" title="Quantum machine">quantum machine</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2011: <a href="/wiki/HPTN_052" title="HPTN 052">HPTN 052</a> clinical trial</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2012: <a href="/wiki/Higgs_boson" title="Higgs boson">Higgs boson</a> discovery</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2013: <a href="/wiki/Cancer_immunotherapy" title="Cancer immunotherapy">Cancer immunotherapy</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2014: <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)"><i>Rosetta</i> comet mission</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2015: <a class="mw-selflink selflink">CRISPR genome-editing method</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2016: <a href="/wiki/First_observation_of_gravitational_waves" title="First observation of gravitational waves">First observation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">gravitational waves</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2017: <a href="/wiki/GW170817" title="GW170817">GW170817</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_merger" title="Neutron star merger">neutron star merger</a>)</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2018: <a href="/wiki/Single-cell_sequencing" title="Single-cell sequencing">Single-cell sequencing</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2019: A <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> <a href="/wiki/Messier_87" title="Messier 87">made visible</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2020: <a href="/wiki/COVID-19_vaccine" title="COVID-19 vaccine">COVID-19 vaccines</a> developed at record speed</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2021: <a href="/wiki/AlphaFold" title="AlphaFold">AI</a> brings <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure_prediction" title="Protein structure prediction">protein structures</a> to all</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2022: <a href="/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope" title="James Webb Space Telescope">James Webb Space Telescope</a> debut</span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2023: <a href="/wiki/GLP-1_receptor_agonist" title="GLP-1 receptor agonist">GLP-1 drugs</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap">2024: <a href="/wiki/Lenacapavir" title="Lenacapavir">Lenacapavir</a></span></li></ul>
